<div id="review_170451003" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/170451003" />
    <a title="Catie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1886642-catie"><img alt="Catie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1385169623p2/1886642.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/170451003?book_show_action=true">May 23, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Catie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Catie" href="/user/show/1886642-catie">Catie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9780545315234" href="/book/show/9647979-beauty-queens">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=audio">audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=read-in-2011">read-in-2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1886642-catie?shelf=ya">ya</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer170451003" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16783636227803209951">This is the book that the other half and I decided to listen to on the combined eighteen hours round trip of driving that we endured last week. I laughed, I giggled, I chortled, I smirked. I listened quietly. I zoned out. I may or may not have fallen asleep for about twenty minutes during track three. I cringed. I looked around desperately for something to pack my ear canals with. I begged for a music break. <br/><br/>“<i>We just have to get through it! We can’t stop now! We’re so damn close….”</i> <br/><br/>“<i>Why do we ha</i></span>
  <span id="freeText16783636227803209951" style="display:none">This is the book that the other half and I decided to listen to on the combined eighteen hours round trip of driving that we endured last week. I laughed, I giggled, I chortled, I smirked. I listened quietly. I zoned out. I may or may not have fallen asleep for about twenty minutes during track three. I cringed. I looked around desperately for something to pack my ear canals with. I begged for a music break. <br /><br />“<i>We just have to get through it! We can’t stop now! We’re so damn close….”</i> <br /><br />“<i>Why do we have a Prius? The bar graph is mocking me! The tortoise like acceleration will never be enough to dispel my rage!</i>”<br /><br />So yeah, this book had some major ups and downs for me. Probably the most satisfying element is the girls’ highly successful survival despite everyone’s low expectations. There are also a lot of gimmicks in this book: footnotes (fifty, exactly), commercial breaks, television/movie previews, and character studies disguised as “fun facts” sheets about each contestant. That sounds negative, but those things were my favorite parts of this book. She really nails the entertainment and beauty industries’ propaganda right between the eyes. <br /><br />The audiobook also earns a special distinction for being one of the best author narrated audiobooks I’ve ever listened to. For those of you who aren’t familiar with audiobooks, that’s actually a big deal. It is a well-known fact among audiobook enthusiasts that authors who decide to narrate their own books tend to majorly FUBAR the narration. Sure, there are a few exceptions (Neil Gaiman can do anything). This is one of them. The footnotes, commercial breaks, and fun facts questionnaires are all produced with hysterical sound effects and music, and Ms. Bray does a wonderful job capturing all the voices. <br /><br />I mean, just to be clear, her accents are <i>atrocious.</i> And there are quite a few in this book: pirate by way of British prep school student, vaguely Australian, Mid-western, Texan, vaguely English, valley girl, and a sort of mish-mash of Pepe Le Pew and Zsa Zsa Gabor on novocaine. Actually, her Texas accent is spot on. But the others…are just awful. However, she throws herself so vigorously into each role; it reminds me exactly of Will Ferrell’s fearless nudity in almost every film of his that I’ve seen. It’s ugly, but also hilarious and brave.<br /><br />The girls have moments of authenticity, but they never transcend beyond caricatures, in my opinion. And she has a nice little panel assembled here: one of these, one of those, can’t forget that one. They all come across more like mouthpieces for various issues. There is a clear message within the pages of this book. It’s so clear that for most of the time I felt like I was being led by one of those little bouncing sing along balls. With a chorus of cheerleaders in the background, spelling it out bodily. And a very wordy fireworks display. With glow in the dark neon sky writing.<br /><br />I absolutely agree with everything that she’s saying, but at a certain point I just wanted to scream, “uncle!” Everything is just so painfully heavy handed. And what really boils my blood is that for all of the loud speechifying and hand-waving, she doesn’t always back up her message with actions. For example, I was initially very absorbed by the story of Mary Lou, a mid-western girl who is taught to fear her own sexuality. This is actually what I would consider to be a major problem in the current YA literature. Girls are portrayed as chaste, innocent dolls who have no interest in sex until that special soul mate type love interest shows up to awaken them like some sort of fairy tale. Even in more aware portrayals, sex is often smothered in flowery metaphor or glossed over.<br /><br />So, I was very excited to see how Mary Lou’s story would progress. But then, she meets Tane. Tane is an eco-warrior and ornithologist (in his teens!) who has perfect looks, is totally accepting of everything, has a kind heart, and perfect bedroom skillz. She actually uses this sentence in her description of Tane: “He smelled of fire smoke, salt breezes, and man.” Which I believe is the customary olfactory description for every male romance novel lead. And then they proceed to engage in some very glossed over, flowery lovin’. And declare their love for each other after about a week.<br /><br />In fact, <i>all</i> of the love interests in this book are paper thin cut-outs of characters without any defining characteristics except their smoking hot bodies and completely accepting hearts. One love interest ends up having some questionable motivations, but then, of course, he reveals himself to be just a wounded puppy with a soft gooey center. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Although, I was pretty happy that he was not given a second chance. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br /><br />When we portray our ideal partners as little more than their looks, it isn’t empowering. It’s degrading. Perhaps the only vaguely realistic romance here is between two of the contestants, but even that seems reduced to a platform that the author uses to discuss sexual orientation.<br /><br />I should probably stop now, as this is getting quite long! I think that some will be able to look past all of the sledgehammer messaging of this book and just enjoy the good, clean fun of it. I just wasn’t able to.<br /><br /><u>Perfect Musical Pairing</u><br /><br />Madonna – <a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=VxIw-kPfxio&amp;feature=related" rel="nofollow noopener">What it Feels Like For a Girl</a><br /><br />I think that this book shows the unrealistic standards that girls are supposed to aspire to very well. Often these are boundaries and molds that we place on ourselves. Girls are supposed to be strong, but not too strong; sexy but chaste; not too white, not too “ethnic;” not too fat, not too thin; not too straight, not too gay; not too dumb, but not too smart either. We’re not supposed to be too much of anything. I am reminded of the lines in this song, <i>“When you open up your mouth to speak, could you be a little weak?”</i> and <i>“When you’re trying hard to be your best, could you be a little less?”</i><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16783636227803209951" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3280677428650585788">This is my first DNF of 2016. I listed to this on audiobook and honestly I'd give the audiobook itself five stars. It's narrated by Libba Bray herself and she's fantastic. The book made me laugh at first, but it got old fast. I know it's supposed to be satire, and I can appreciate that, but I just can't with these girls anymore.</span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('W9j6sZWCguT7GnjYnYqEkq4793ySu+EFU01Ve0ZyzCZ7Wxmp9HuejP0uX79/B7fShlrrRGVN2Jnp4l9CAU3WEQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ECA+pf/RCCBjUiHZWU9zDm2rFMvYZGhti/2Y1PsQmVYwo929nigUSGVmBr67wkBORcoI8y+SUfExUpLtvC+DYQ==')})
  }
//]]>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/2526.Libba_Bray">About Libba Bray</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/2526.Libba_Bray"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1206563678p3/2526.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/2526.Libba_Bray">Libba Bray
</a></div>
<div class='goodreadsAuthorBadge'></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
14,515
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer5939092670069840623">What is it about writing an author bio that gives me that deer-in-headlights feeling? It's not exactly like I'm going to say "I was born in Alabama…" and somebody's going to jump up and snarl, "Oh yeah? Prove it!" At least I hope not.<br/><br/>I think what gets me feeling itchy is all that emphasis on the facts of a life, while all the juicy, relevant, human oddity stuff gets left on the cutting room floor.</span>
  <span id="freeText5939092670069840623" style="display:none">What is it about writing an author bio that gives me that deer-in-headlights feeling? It's not exactly like I'm going to say "I was born in Alabama…" and somebody's going to jump up and snarl, "Oh yeah? Prove it!" At least I hope not.<br /><br />I think what gets me feeling itchy is all that emphasis on the facts of a life, while all the juicy, relevant, human oddity stuff gets left on the cutting room floor. I could tell you the facts–I lived in Texas for most of my life; I live in New York City with my husband and six-year-old son now; I have freckles and a lopsided smile; I'm allergic to penicillin.<br /><br />But that doesn't really give you much insight into me. That doesn't tell you that I stuck a bead up my nose while watching TV when I was four and thought I'd have to go to the ER and have it cut out. Or that I once sang a punk version of "Que Sera Sera" onstage in New York City. Or that I made everyone call me "Bert" in ninth grade for no reason that I can think of. See what I mean?<br /><br />God is in the details. So with that in mind, here is my bio. Sort of.<br /><br /><br />TEN THINGS YOU DON'T KNOW ABOUT ME by Libba Bray<br /><br />1. I lived in Texas until I was 26 years old, then I moved to New York City with $600.00 in my shoe ('cause muggers won't take it out of your shoe, y'know . . . riiiiight . . .) and a punchbowl (my grandmother's gift) under my arm. I ended up using the punchbowl box as an end table for two years.<br /><br />2. My dad was a Presbyterian minister. Yes, I am one of those dreaded P.K.s–Preacher's Kids. Be afraid. Be very afraid . . .<br /><br />3. The first story I ever wrote, in Mrs. McBee's 6th grade English class, was about a girl whose family is kidnapped and held hostage by a murderous lot of bank robbers who intend to kill the whole family–including the dog–until the 12-year-old heroine foils the plot and saves the day. It included colored pencil illustrations of manly-looking, bearded criminals smoking, and, oblivious to the fact that The Beatles had already sort of laid claim to the title, I called my novel, HELP. My mom still has a copy. And when I do something she doesn't like, she threatens to find it.<br /><br />4. My favorite word is "redemption." I like both its meaning and the sound. My least favorite word is "maybe." "Maybe" is almost always a "no" drawn out in cruel fashion.<br /><br />5. My three worst habits are overeating, self-doubt, and the frequent use of the "f" word.<br /><br />6. The three things I like best about myself are my sense of humor, my ability to listen, and my imagination.<br /><br />7. I have an artificial left eye. I lost my real eye in a car accident when I was eighteen. In fact, I had to have my entire face rebuilt because I smashed it up pretty good. It took six years and thirteen surgeries. However, I did have the pleasure of freezing a plastic eyeball in an ice cube, putting it in a friend's drink, ("Eyeball in your highball?") and watching him freak completely. Okay, so maybe that's not going down on my good karma record. But it sure was fun.<br /><br />8. In 7th grade, my three best friends and I dressed up as KISS and walked around our neighborhood on Halloween. Man, we were such dorks.<br /><br />9. I once spent New Year's Eve in a wetsuit. I'd gone to the party in a black dress that was a little too tight (too many holiday cookies) and when I went to sit down, the dress ripped up the back completely. Can we all say, mortified? The problem was, my friends were moving out of their house–everything was packed and on a truck–and there was nothing I could put on . . . but a wetsuit that they still had tacked to the wall. I spent the rest of the party maneuvering through throngs of people feeling like a giant squid.<br /><br />10. I got married in Florence, Italy. My husband and I were in love but totally broke, so we eloped and got married in Italy, where he was going on a business trip. We had to pull a guy off the street to be our witness. It was incredibly romantic.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5939092670069840623" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
